From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

(Redirected from [Cardiovascular system](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cardiovascular_system&redirect=no "Cardiovascular system"))

Organ system for circulating blood in animals

For other uses, see [Circulatory system (disambiguation)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circulatory_system_(disambiguation) "Circulatory system (disambiguation)").Several terms redirect here. For other uses, see [Bloodstream (song)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bloodstream_(song) "Bloodstream (song)") and [Cardio-Vascular (album)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardio-Vascular_(album) "Cardio-Vascular (album)").

| --- |
| [![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Circulatory_System_en.svg/330px-Circulatory_System_en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Circulatory_System_en.svg) The human circulatory system (simplified). Blue indicates [deoxygenated blood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deoxygenated_blood "Deoxygenated blood") carried in [veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein "Vein"). | Identifiers |
| [MeSH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medical_Subject_Headings "Medical Subject Headings") | [D002319](https://meshb.nlm.nih.gov/record/ui?ui=D002319) |
| [FMA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundational_Model_of_Anatomy "Foundational Model of Anatomy") | [7161](https://bioportal.bioontology.org/ontologies/FMA/?p=classes&conceptid=http%3A%2F%2Fpurl.org%2Fsig%2Font%2Ffma%2Ffma7161) |
| [Anatomical terminology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomical_terminology "Anatomical terminology") [[edit on Wikidata](https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q11068 "d:Q11068")] |

In [vertebrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebrate "Vertebrate"), the **circulatory system** is a [system of organs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organ_system "Organ system") that includes the [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart"), [blood vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_vessel "Blood vessel"), and [blood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood "Blood") which is circulated throughout the body.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Hall-1)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Saladin2-2) It includes the **cardiovascular system**, or **vascular system**, that consists of the heart and blood vessels (from Greek _kardia_ meaning _heart_, and Latin _vascula_ meaning _vessels_). The circulatory system has two divisions, a [systemic circulation or circuit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Systemic_circulation "Systemic circulation"), and a [pulmonary circulation or circuit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Saladin3-3) Some sources use the terms _cardiovascular system_ and _vascular system_ interchangeably with _circulatory system_.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-ncbibooks-4)

The network of blood vessels are the [great vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_vessels "Great vessels") of the heart including large [elastic arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elastic_arteries "Elastic arteries"), and large [veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein "Vein"); other arteries, smaller [arterioles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arteriole "Arteriole"), [capillaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillaries "Capillaries") that join with [venules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venule "Venule") (small veins), and other veins. The [circulatory system is closed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed_circulatory_system "Closed circulatory system") in vertebrates, which means that the blood never leaves the network of blood vessels. Many [invertebrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrate "Invertebrate") such as [arthropods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthropod "Arthropod") have an [open circulatory system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_circulatory_system "Open circulatory system") with a heart that pumps a hemolymph which returns via the body cavity rather than via blood vessels. [Diploblasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diploblast "Diploblast") such as [sponges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sponges "Sponges") and [comb jellies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comb_jellies "Comb jellies") lack a circulatory system.

Blood is a [fluid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_fluid "Body fluid") consisting of [plasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_plasma "Blood plasma"), [red blood cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_blood_cell "Red blood cell"), [white blood cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_blood_cell "White blood cell"), and [platelets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platelets "Platelets"); it is circulated around the body carrying [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") and [nutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrient "Nutrient") to the tissues and collecting and disposing of [waste materials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_waste "Metabolic waste"). Circulated nutrients include [proteins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_(nutrient) "Protein (nutrient)") and [minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient) "Mineral (nutrient)") and other components include [hemoglobin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemoglobin "Hemoglobin"), [hormones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hormone "Hormone"), and [gases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas "Gas") such as oxygen and [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide"). These substances provide nourishment, help the [immune system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system "Immune system") to fight [diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diseases "Diseases"), and help maintain [homeostasis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homeostasis "Homeostasis") by [stabilizing temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoregulation "Thermoregulation") and [natural pH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PH#pH_of_various_body_fluids "PH").

In vertebrates, the [lymphatic system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_system "Lymphatic system") is complementary to the circulatory system. The lymphatic system carries excess plasma ([filtered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillary_filtration "Capillary filtration") from the circulatory system [capillaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillaries "Capillaries") as [interstitial fluid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstitial_fluid "Interstitial fluid") between cells) away from the body tissues via accessory routes that return excess fluid back to blood circulation as [lymph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymph "Lymph").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Sherwood,_Lauralee_2011_401-5) The lymphatic system is a subsystem that is essential for the functioning of the blood circulatory system; without it the blood would become depleted of fluid.

The lymphatic system also works with the immune system.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Saladin4-6) The circulation of lymph takes much longer than that of blood[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-CRU-7) and, unlike the closed (blood) circulatory system, the lymphatic system is an open system. Some sources describe it as a _secondary circulatory system_.

The circulatory system can be affected by many [cardiovascular diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease "Cardiovascular disease"). [Cardiologists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiologist "Cardiologist") are medical professionals which specialise in the heart, and [cardiothoracic surgeons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiothoracic_surgeon "Cardiothoracic surgeon") specialise in operating on the heart and its surrounding areas. [Vascular surgeons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_surgeon "Vascular surgeon") focus on disorders of the blood vessels, and lymphatic vessels.

Structure
---------

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/2101_Blood_Flow_Through_the_Heart.jpg/500px-2101_Blood_Flow_Through_the_Heart.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2101_Blood_Flow_Through_the_Heart.jpg)

Blood flow in the pulmonary and systemic circulations showing capillary networks in the torso sections

The circulatory system includes the [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart"), [blood vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_vessel "Blood vessel"), and [blood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood "Blood").[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Saladin2-2) The **cardiovascular system** in all vertebrates, consists of the heart and blood vessels. The circulatory system is further divided into two major circuits – a [pulmonary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation"), and a [systemic circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Systemic_circulation "Systemic circulation").[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-8)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Hall-1)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Saladin3-3) The pulmonary circulation is a circuit loop from the [right heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_heart "Right heart") taking deoxygenated blood to the [lungs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lung "Lung") where it is oxygenated and returned to the [left heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_heart "Left heart"). The systemic circulation is a circuit loop that delivers oxygenated blood from the left heart to the rest of the body, and returns deoxygenated blood back to the right heart via [large veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_vessels "Great vessels") known as the [venae cavae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venae_cavae "Venae cavae"). The systemic circulation can also be defined as two parts – a _macrocirculation_ and a _[microcirculation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microcirculation "Microcirculation")_. An average adult contains five to six quarts (roughly 4.7 to 5.7 liters) of blood, accounting for approximately 7% of their total body weight.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-9) Blood consists of [plasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_plasma "Blood plasma"), [red blood cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_blood_cells "Red blood cells"), [white blood cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_blood_cells "White blood cells"), and [platelets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platelets "Platelets"). The [digestive system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digestive_system "Digestive system") also works with the circulatory system to provide the nutrients the system needs to keep the [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart") pumping.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Guyton-10)

Further circulatory routes are associated, such as the [coronary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_circulation "Coronary circulation") to the heart itself, the [cerebral circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebral_circulation "Cerebral circulation") to the [brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain "Brain"), [renal circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renal_circulation "Renal circulation") to the [kidneys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kidney "Kidney"), and [bronchial circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchial_circulation "Bronchial circulation") to the [bronchi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchi "Bronchi") in the lungs. The human circulatory system is [closed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed_circulatory_system "Closed circulatory system"), meaning that the blood is contained within the [vascular network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_vessels "Blood vessels").[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Lawton-11) Nutrients travel through tiny blood vessels of the microcirculation to reach organs.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Lawton-11) The [lymphatic system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_system "Lymphatic system") is an essential subsystem of the circulatory system consisting of a network of [lymphatic vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_vessel "Lymphatic vessel"), [lymph nodes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymph_nodes "Lymph nodes"), [organs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_organs "Lymphatic organs"), [tissues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_tissues "Lymphatic tissues") and circulating [lymph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymph "Lymph"). This subsystem is an [open system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_circulatory_system "Open circulatory system").[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Gartner-12) A major function is to carry the lymph, draining and returning [interstitial fluid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstitial_fluid "Interstitial fluid") into the [lymphatic ducts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_duct "Lymphatic duct") back to the heart for return to the circulatory system. Another [major function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_system#Function "Lymphatic system") is working together with the [immune system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system "Immune system") to provide defense against [pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen").[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Alberts-13)

### Heart

Main article: [Heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart")

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Blood_oxygenation_to_the_pulmonary_and_systemic_circulation.svg/250px-Blood_oxygenation_to_the_pulmonary_and_systemic_circulation.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Blood_oxygenation_to_the_pulmonary_and_systemic_circulation.svg)

Diagram of the human [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart") showing blood oxygenation to the pulmonary and systemic circulation

The heart pumps blood to all parts of the body providing [nutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrient "Nutrient") and [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") to every [cell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology) "Cell (biology)"), and removing waste products. The left heart pumps oxygenated blood returned from the lungs to the rest of the body in the [systemic circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Systemic_circulation "Systemic circulation"). The right heart pumps deoxygenated blood to the lungs in the [pulmonary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation"). In the human heart there is one [atrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atrium_(heart) "Atrium (heart)") and one [ventricle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ventricle_(heart) "Ventricle (heart)") for each circulation, and with both a systemic and a pulmonary circulation there are four chambers in total: [left atrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_atrium "Left atrium"), [left ventricle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_ventricle "Left ventricle"), [right atrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_atrium "Right atrium") and [right ventricle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_ventricle "Right ventricle"). The right atrium is the upper chamber of the right side of the heart. The blood that is returned to the right atrium is deoxygenated (poor in oxygen) and passed into the right ventricle to be pumped through the pulmonary artery to the lungs for re-oxygenation and removal of carbon dioxide. The left atrium receives newly oxygenated blood from the lungs as well as the pulmonary vein which is passed into the strong left ventricle to be pumped through the aorta to the different organs of the body.

#### Pulmonary circulation

Main article: [Pulmonary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation")

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/2119_Pulmonary_Circuit.jpg/500px-2119_Pulmonary_Circuit.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2119_Pulmonary_Circuit.jpg)

The [pulmonary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation") as it passes from the [heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart "Heart"). Showing both the [pulmonary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_artery "Pulmonary artery") and [bronchial arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchial_artery "Bronchial artery").

The pulmonary circulation is the part of the circulatory system in which [oxygen-depleted blood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deoxygenated_blood "Deoxygenated blood") is pumped away from the heart, via the [pulmonary artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_artery "Pulmonary artery"), to the [lungs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lungs "Lungs") and returned, oxygenated, to the heart via the [pulmonary vein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_vein "Pulmonary vein").

Oxygen-deprived blood from the superior and inferior [vena cava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vena_cava "Vena cava") enters the right atrium of the heart and flows through the [tricuspid valve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tricuspid_valve "Tricuspid valve") (right atrioventricular valve) into the right ventricle, from which it is then pumped through the [pulmonary semilunar valve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_semilunar_valve "Pulmonary semilunar valve") into the pulmonary artery to the lungs. [Gas exchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_exchange "Gas exchange") occurs in the lungs, whereby [CO 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") is released from the blood, and oxygen is absorbed. The pulmonary vein returns the now oxygen-rich blood to the [left atrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_atrium "Left atrium").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Guyton-10)

A separate circuit from the systemic circulation, the [bronchial circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchial_circulation "Bronchial circulation") supplies blood to the tissue of the larger airways of the lung.

#### Systemic circulation

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/2105_Capillary_Bed.jpg/500px-2105_Capillary_Bed.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2105_Capillary_Bed.jpg)

Capillary bed

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Capillary_system_CERT.jpg/250px-Capillary_system_CERT.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Capillary_system_CERT.jpg)

Diagram of capillary network joining the arterial system with the venous system

The systemic circulation is a circuit loop that delivers oxygenated blood from the left heart to the rest of the body through the [aorta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aorta "Aorta"). Deoxygenated blood is returned in the systemic circulation to the right heart via two large veins, the [inferior vena cava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava "Inferior vena cava") and [superior vena cava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superior_vena_cava "Superior vena cava"), where it is pumped from the right atrium into the pulmonary circulation for oxygenation. The systemic circulation can also be defined as having two parts – a macrocirculation and a [microcirculation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microcirculation "Microcirculation").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Guyton-10)

### Blood vessels

The [blood vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_vessel "Blood vessel") of the circulatory system are the [arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arteries "Arteries"), [veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veins "Veins"), and [capillaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillaries "Capillaries"). The large arteries and veins that take blood to, and away from the heart are known as the [great vessels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_vessels "Great vessels").[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Gray's-14)

#### Arteries

Main article: [Artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artery "Artery")

See also: [Arterial tree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arterial_tree "Arterial tree")

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Vein_art_near.png/250px-Vein_art_near.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vein_art_near.png)

Depiction of the heart, major veins and arteries constructed from body scans

Oxygenated blood enters the systemic circulation when leaving the left ventricle, via the [aortic semilunar valve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aortic_valve "Aortic valve").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Iaizzo-15) The first part of the systemic circulation is the aorta, a massive and thick-walled artery. The aorta arches and gives branches supplying the upper part of the body after passing through the aortic opening of the diaphragm at the level of thoracic ten vertebra, it enters the abdomen.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Iaizzo_two-16) Later, it descends down and supplies branches to abdomen, pelvis, perineum and the lower limbs.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Iaizzo_three-17)

The walls of the aorta are elastic. This elasticity helps to maintain the [blood pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_pressure "Blood pressure") throughout the body.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Vaz-18) When the aorta receives almost five litres of blood from the heart, it recoils and is responsible for pulsating blood pressure. As the aorta branches into smaller arteries, their elasticity goes on decreasing and their compliance goes on increasing.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Vaz-18)

#### Capillaries

Arteries branch into small passages called [arterioles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arteriole "Arteriole") and then into the [capillaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillary "Capillary").[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-19) The capillaries merge to bring blood into the venous system.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-20) The total length of muscle capillaries in a 70 kg human is estimated to be between 9,000 and 19,000 km.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-21)

#### Veins

Main article: [Vein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein "Vein")

Capillaries merge into [venules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venule "Venule"), which merge into veins.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Mcconnell-22) The [venous system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venous_system "Venous system") feeds into the two major veins: the superior vena cava – which mainly drains tissues above the heart – and the inferior vena cava – which mainly drains tissues below the heart. These two large veins empty into the right atrium of the heart.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Floyd-23)

#### Portal veins

Main article: [Portal venous system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portal_venous_system "Portal venous system")

The general rule is that arteries from the heart branch out into capillaries, which collect into veins leading back to the heart. [Portal veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portal_venous_system "Portal venous system") are a slight exception to this. In humans, the only significant example is the [hepatic portal vein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hepatic_portal_vein "Hepatic portal vein") which combines from capillaries around the [gastrointestinal tract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract "Gastrointestinal tract") where the blood absorbs the various products of digestion; rather than leading directly back to the heart, the hepatic portal vein branches into a second capillary system in the [liver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liver "Liver").

#### Coronary circulation

Main article: [Coronary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_circulation "Coronary circulation")

The heart itself is supplied with oxygen and nutrients through a small "loop" of the systemic circulation and derives very little from the blood contained within the four chambers. The coronary circulation system provides a blood supply to the [heart muscle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myocardium "Myocardium") itself. The coronary circulation begins near the origin of the aorta by two [coronary arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_arteries "Coronary arteries"): the [right coronary artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_coronary_artery "Right coronary artery") and the [left coronary artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left_coronary_artery "Left coronary artery"). After nourishing the heart muscle, blood returns through the coronary veins into the [coronary sinus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_sinus "Coronary sinus") and from this one into the right atrium. Backflow of blood through its opening during [atrial systole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atrial_systole "Atrial systole") is prevented by the [Thebesian valve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thebesian_valve "Thebesian valve"). The [smallest cardiac veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smallest_cardiac_veins "Smallest cardiac veins") drain directly into the heart chambers.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Guyton-10)

#### Cerebral circulation

Main article: [Cerebral circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebral_circulation "Cerebral circulation")

The brain has a dual blood supply, an _anterior_ and a _posterior circulation_ from arteries at its front and back. The anterior circulation arises from the [internal carotid arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_carotid_artery "Internal carotid artery") to supply the front of the brain. The posterior circulation arises from the [vertebral arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebral_artery "Vertebral artery"), to supply the back of the brain and [brainstem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brainstem "Brainstem"). The circulation from the front and the back join ([anastomise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anastomise "Anastomise")) at the [circle of Willis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle_of_Willis "Circle of Willis"). The [neurovascular unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurovascular_unit "Neurovascular unit"), composed of various cells and vasculature channels within the brain, regulates the flow of blood to activated neurons in order to satisfy their high energy demands.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-24)

#### Renal circulation

The [renal circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renal_circulation "Renal circulation") is the blood supply to the [kidneys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kidney "Kidney"), contains many specialized blood vessels and receives around 20% of the cardiac output. It branches from the [abdominal aorta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdominal_aorta "Abdominal aorta") and returns blood to the ascending [inferior vena cava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inferior_vena_cava "Inferior vena cava").

Development
-----------

Main articles: [Heart development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_development "Heart development"), [Vasculogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasculogenesis "Vasculogenesis"), [Vascular remodelling in the embryo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_remodelling_in_the_embryo "Vascular remodelling in the embryo"), and [Fetal circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fetal_circulation "Fetal circulation")

The development of the circulatory system starts with [vasculogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasculogenesis "Vasculogenesis") in the [embryo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embryo "Embryo"). The human arterial and venous systems develop from different areas in the embryo. The arterial system develops mainly from the [aortic arches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aortic_arches "Aortic arches"), six pairs of arches that develop on the upper part of the embryo. The venous system arises from three bilateral veins during weeks 4– 8 of [embryogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_embryogenesis "Human embryogenesis"). [Fetal circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fetal_circulation "Fetal circulation") begins within the 8th week of development. Fetal circulation does not include the lungs, which are bypassed via the [truncus arteriosus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncus_arteriosus "Truncus arteriosus"). Before birth the [fetus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fetus "Fetus") obtains [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") (and [nutrients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nutrient "Nutrient")) from the mother through the [placenta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Placenta "Placenta") and the [umbilical cord](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umbilical_cord "Umbilical cord").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Whitaker-25)

### Arteries

Main article: [Aortic arches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aortic_arches "Aortic arches")

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Erytrocyte_deoxy_to_oxy_v0.7.gif/250px-Erytrocyte_deoxy_to_oxy_v0.7.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Erytrocyte_deoxy_to_oxy_v0.7.gif)

Animation of a typical human red blood cell cycle in the circulatory system. This animation occurs at a faster rate (~20 seconds of the average [60-second cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_blood_cell#Human "Red blood cell")) and shows the red blood cell deforming as it enters capillaries, as well as the bars changing color as the cell alternates in states of oxygenation along the circulatory system.

The human arterial system originates from the [aortic arches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aortic_arches "Aortic arches") and from the [dorsal aortae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dorsal_aortae "Dorsal aortae") starting from week 4 of embryonic life. The first and second aortic arches regress and form only the [maxillary arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxillary_artery "Maxillary artery") and [stapedial arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stapedial_artery "Stapedial artery") respectively. The arterial system itself arises from aortic arches 3, 4 and 6 (aortic arch 5 completely regresses).

The dorsal aortae, present on the [dorsal](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dorsal "wikt:dorsal") side of the embryo, are initially present on both sides of the embryo. They later fuse to form the basis for the aorta itself. Approximately thirty smaller arteries branch from this at the back and sides. These branches form the [intercostal arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercostal_arteries "Intercostal arteries"), arteries of the arms and legs, lumbar arteries and the lateral sacral arteries. Branches to the sides of the aorta will form the definitive [renal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renal_artery "Renal artery"), [suprarenal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inferior_suprarenal_artery "Inferior suprarenal artery") and [gonadal arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gonadal_artery "Gonadal artery"). Finally, branches at the front of the aorta consist of the [vitelline arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitelline_arteries "Vitelline arteries") and [umbilical arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umbilical_arteries "Umbilical arteries"). The vitelline arteries form the [celiac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celiac_artery "Celiac artery"), [superior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superior_mesenteric_artery "Superior mesenteric artery") and [inferior mesenteric arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inferior_mesenteric_artery "Inferior mesenteric artery") of the gastrointestinal tract. After birth, the umbilical arteries will form the [internal iliac arteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_iliac_artery "Internal iliac artery").

### Veins

Further information: [Vasculogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasculogenesis "Vasculogenesis")

The human venous system develops mainly from the [vitelline veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitelline_vein "Vitelline vein"), the [umbilical veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umbilical_vein "Umbilical vein") and the [cardinal veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Posterior_cardinal_vein "Posterior cardinal vein"), all of which empty into the [sinus venosus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinus_venosus "Sinus venosus").

Function
--------

Main article: [Blood §Oxygen transport](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood#Oxygen_transport "Blood")

About 98.5% of the oxygen in a sample of [arterial blood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arterial_blood "Arterial blood") in a healthy human, breathing air at sea-level pressure, is chemically combined with [hemoglobin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemoglobin "Hemoglobin") molecules. About 1.5% is physically dissolved in the other blood liquids and not connected to hemoglobin. The hemoglobin molecule is the primary transporter of oxygen in vertebrates.

Clinical significance
---------------------

Further information: [List of circulatory system conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_circulatory_system_conditions "List of circulatory system conditions")

Many diseases affect the circulatory system. These include a number of [cardiovascular diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease "Cardiovascular disease"), affecting the heart and blood vessels; [hematologic diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hematologic_disease "Hematologic disease") that affect the blood, such as [anemia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anemia "Anemia"), and [lymphatic diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphatic_disease "Lymphatic disease") affecting the lymphatic system. [Cardiologists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiologist "Cardiologist") are medical professionals which specialise in the heart, and [cardiothoracic surgeons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiothoracic_surgeon "Cardiothoracic surgeon") specialise in operating on the heart and its surrounding areas. [Vascular surgeons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_surgeon "Vascular surgeon") focus on the blood vessels.

### Cardiovascular disease

Main article: [Cardiovascular disease](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease "Cardiovascular disease")

Diseases affecting the cardiovascular system are called _cardiovascular disease_.

Many of these diseases are called "[lifestyle diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lifestyle_disease "Lifestyle disease")" because they develop over time and are related to a person's exercise habits, diet, whether they smoke, and other lifestyle choices a person makes. [Atherosclerosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atherosclerosis "Atherosclerosis") is the precursor to many of these diseases. It is where small [atheromatous plaques](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atheroma "Atheroma") build up in the walls of medium and large arteries. This may eventually grow or rupture to occlude the arteries. It is also a risk factor for [acute coronary syndromes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acute_coronary_syndrome "Acute coronary syndrome"), which are diseases that are characterised by a sudden deficit of oxygenated blood to the heart tissue. Atherosclerosis is also associated with problems such as [aneurysm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aneurysm "Aneurysm") formation or splitting ("dissection") of arteries.

Another major cardiovascular disease involves the creation of a [clot, called a "thrombus"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thrombus "Thrombus"). These can originate in veins or arteries. [Deep venous thrombosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_venous_thrombosis "Deep venous thrombosis"), which mostly occurs in the legs, is one cause of clots in the veins of the legs, particularly when a person has been stationary for a long time. These clots may [embolise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embolus "Embolus"), meaning travel to another location in the body. The results of this may include [pulmonary embolus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_embolus "Pulmonary embolus"), [transient ischaemic attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transient_ischaemic_attack "Transient ischaemic attack"), or [stroke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stroke "Stroke").

Cardiovascular diseases may also be congenital in nature, such as [heart defects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congenital_heart_defect "Congenital heart defect") or [persistent fetal circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persistent_fetal_circulation "Persistent fetal circulation"), where the circulatory changes that are supposed to happen after birth do not. Not all congenital changes to the circulatory system are associated with diseases, a large number are [anatomical variations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomical_variation "Anatomical variation").

### Investigations

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Arteria-lusoria_MRA_MIP.gif/250px-Arteria-lusoria_MRA_MIP.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Arteria-lusoria_MRA_MIP.gif)

[Magnetic resonance angiography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_angiography "Magnetic resonance angiography") of [aberrant subclavian artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aberrant_subclavian_artery "Aberrant subclavian artery")

The function and health of the circulatory system and its parts are measured in a variety of manual and automated ways. These include simple methods such as those that are part of the [cardiovascular examination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_examination "Cardiovascular examination"), including the taking of a person's [pulse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulse "Pulse") as an indicator of a person's [heart rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_rate "Heart rate"), the taking of [blood pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blood_pressure "Blood pressure") through a [sphygmomanometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphygmomanometer "Sphygmomanometer") or the use of a [stethoscope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stethoscope "Stethoscope") to listen to the heart for [murmurs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiac_murmur "Cardiac murmur") which may indicate problems with the [heart's valves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_valve "Heart valve"). An [electrocardiogram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrocardiogram "Electrocardiogram") can also be used to evaluate the way in which electricity is conducted through the heart.

Other more invasive means can also be used. A [cannula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cannula "Cannula") or [catheter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catheter "Catheter") inserted into an artery may be used to measure [pulse pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulse_pressure "Pulse pressure") or [pulmonary wedge pressures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_wedge_pressure "Pulmonary wedge pressure"). Angiography, which involves injecting a dye into an artery to visualise an arterial tree, can be used in the heart ([coronary angiography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_angiography "Coronary angiography")) or brain. At the same time as the arteries are visualised, blockages or narrowings may be fixed through the insertion of [stents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stent "Stent"), and active bleeds may be managed by the insertion of coils. An MRI may be used to image arteries, called an [MRI angiogram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_angiography "Magnetic resonance angiography"). For evaluation of the blood supply to the lungs a [CT pulmonary angiogram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CT_pulmonary_angiogram "CT pulmonary angiogram") may be used. [Vascular ultrasonography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_ultrasonography#Blood_vessels "Doppler ultrasonography") may be used to investigate [vascular diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_disease "Vascular disease") affecting the [venous system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venous_system "Venous system") and the [arterial system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arterial_system "Arterial system") including the diagnosis of [stenosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stenosis "Stenosis"), [thrombosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultrasonography_of_deep_venous_thrombosis "Ultrasonography of deep venous thrombosis") or [venous insufficiency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultrasonography_of_chronic_venous_insufficiency_of_the_legs "Ultrasonography of chronic venous insufficiency of the legs"). An [intravascular ultrasound](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intravascular_ultrasound "Intravascular ultrasound") using a [catheter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catheter "Catheter") is also an option.

### Surgery

[![Image 28: [icon]](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg)This section **needs expansion**. You can help by [making an edit request](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Talk%3ACirculatory_system&preload=Template%3ASubmit+an+edit+request%2Fpreload&action=edit&section=new&editintro=Template%3AEdit+protected%2Feditintro&preloadtitle=Protected+edit+request+on+16+August+2025&preloadparams%5B%5D=edit+fully-protected&preloadparams%5B%5D=Circulatory+system)[adding to it](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Circulatory_system&action=edit&section=). _(March 2015)_

Further information: [Vascular surgery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_surgery "Vascular surgery") and [Vascular bypass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_bypass "Vascular bypass")

There are a number of surgical procedures performed on the circulatory system:

*   [Coronary artery bypass surgery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery "Coronary artery bypass surgery")
*   [Coronary stent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_stent "Coronary stent") used in [angioplasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angioplasty "Angioplasty")
*   [Vascular surgery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_surgery "Vascular surgery")
*   [Vein stripping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein_stripping "Vein stripping")
*   Cosmetic procedures

Cardiovascular procedures are more likely to be performed in an inpatient setting than in an ambulatory care setting; in the United States, only 28% of cardiovascular surgeries were performed in the ambulatory care setting.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-26)

Other animals
-------------

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Open_Circulatroy.gif/250px-Open_Circulatroy.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Open_Circulatroy.gif)

The open circulatory system of the grasshopper – made up of a heart, vessels and hemolymph. The hemolymph is pumped through the heart, into the aorta, dispersed into the head and throughout the hemocoel, then back through the ostia in the heart and the process repeated.

While humans, as well as other [vertebrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertebrates "Vertebrates"), have a closed blood circulatory system (meaning that the blood never leaves the network of arteries, veins and capillaries), some [invertebrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invertebrate "Invertebrate") groups have an open circulatory system containing a heart but limited blood vessels. The most primitive, [diploblastic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diploblastic "Diploblastic") animal [phyla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylum "Phylum") lack circulatory systems.

An additional transport system, the lymphatic system, which is only found in animals with a closed blood circulation, is an open system providing an accessory route for excess interstitial fluid to be returned to the blood.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Sherwood,_Lauralee_2011_401-5)

The blood vascular system first appeared probably in an ancestor of the [triploblasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triploblasts "Triploblasts") over 600 million years ago, overcoming the time-distance constraints of diffusion, while [endothelium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endothelium "Endothelium") evolved in an ancestral vertebrate some 540–510 million years ago.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-27)

### Open circulatory system

See also: [Hemolymph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemolymph "Hemolymph")

In [arthropods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthropod "Arthropod"), the open circulatory system is a system in which a fluid in a [cavity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_cavity "Body cavity") called the **hemocoel** or **haemocoel** bathes the organs directly with oxygen and nutrients, with there being no distinction between blood and interstitial fluid; this combined fluid is called [hemolymph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemolymph "Hemolymph") or haemolymph.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-28) Muscular movements by the animal during [locomotion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_locomotion "Animal locomotion") can facilitate hemolymph movement, but diverting flow from one area to another is limited. When the heart relaxes, blood is drawn back toward the heart through open-ended pores (ostia).

Hemolymph fills all of the interior hemocoel of the body and surrounds all [cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology) "Cell (biology)"). Hemolymph is composed of [water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water "Water"), [inorganic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry "Inorganic chemistry")[salts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_(chemistry) "Salt (chemistry)") (mostly [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium "Sodium"), [chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloride "Chloride"), [potassium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium "Potassium"), [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"), and [calcium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium "Calcium")), and [organic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_chemistry "Organic chemistry") (mostly carbohydrates, [proteins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein"), and [lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid "Lipid")). The primary oxygen transporter molecule is [hemocyanin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemocyanin "Hemocyanin").

There are free-floating cells, the [hemocytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemocyte_(invertebrate_immune_system_cell) "Hemocyte (invertebrate immune system cell)"), within the hemolymph. They play a role in the arthropod [immune system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system "Immune system").

[![Image 30](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Pseudoceros_liparus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg/250px-Pseudoceros_liparus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pseudoceros_liparus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg)

Flatworms, such as this _[Pseudoceros bifurcus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudoceros\_bifurcus "Pseudoceros bifurcus")_, lack specialized circulatory organs.

### Closed circulatory system

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/250px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Two_chamber_heart.svg)

Two-chambered heart of a fish

The circulatory systems of all vertebrates, as well as of [annelids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annelid "Annelid") (for example, [earthworms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthworm "Earthworm")) and [cephalopods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cephalopod "Cephalopod") ([squids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squid "Squid"), [octopuses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octopus "Octopus") and relatives) always keep their circulating blood enclosed within heart chambers or blood vessels and are classified as _closed_, just as in humans. Still, the systems of [fish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish "Fish"), [amphibians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian "Amphibian"), [reptiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reptile "Reptile"), and [birds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird "Bird") show various stages of the [evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution") of the circulatory system.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-29) Closed systems permit blood to be directed to the organs that require it.

In fish, the system has only one circuit, with the blood being pumped through the capillaries of the [gills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gill "Gill") and on to the capillaries of the body tissues. This is known as _single cycle_ circulation. The heart of fish is, therefore, only a single pump (consisting of two chambers).[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In amphibians and most reptiles, a double circulatory system is used, but the heart is not always completely separated into two pumps. Amphibians have a three-chambered heart.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In reptiles, the [ventricular septum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ventricular_septum "Ventricular septum") of the heart is incomplete and the [pulmonary artery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_artery "Pulmonary artery") is equipped with a [sphincter muscle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphincter_muscle "Sphincter muscle"). This allows a second possible route of blood flow. Instead of blood flowing through the pulmonary artery to the lungs, the sphincter may be contracted to divert this blood flow through the incomplete ventricular septum into the left ventricle and out through the [aorta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aorta "Aorta"). This means the blood flows from the capillaries to the heart and back to the capillaries instead of to the lungs. This process is useful to [ectothermic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ectothermic "Ectothermic") (cold-blooded) animals in the regulation of their body temperature.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

Mammals, birds and [crocodilians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crocodilia "Crocodilia") show complete separation of the heart into two pumps, for a total of four heart chambers; it is thought that the four-chambered heart of birds and crocodilians evolved independently from that of mammals.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-30) Double circulatory systems permit blood to be repressurized after returning from the lungs, speeding up delivery of oxygen to tissues.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

### No circulatory system

Circulatory systems are absent in some animals, including [flatworms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flatworm "Flatworm"). Their [body cavity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_cavity "Body cavity") has no lining or enclosed fluid. Instead, a muscular [pharynx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pharynx "Pharynx") leads to an extensively branched [digestive system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digestive_system "Digestive system") that facilitates direct [diffusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffusion "Diffusion") of nutrients to all cells. The flatworm's dorso-ventrally flattened body shape also restricts the distance of any cell from the digestive system or the exterior of the organism. [Oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") can diffuse from the surrounding water into the cells, and carbon dioxide can diffuse out. Consequently, every cell is able to obtain nutrients, water and oxygen without the need of a transport system.

Some animals, such as [jellyfish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jellyfish "Jellyfish"), have more extensive branching from their [gastrovascular cavity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastrovascular_cavity "Gastrovascular cavity") (which functions as both a place of digestion and a form of circulation), this branching allows for bodily fluids to reach the outer layers, since the digestion begins in the inner layers.

History
-------

[![Image 32](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/250px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit,_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam,_1543..JPG)

Human anatomical chart of blood vessels, with heart, lungs, liver and kidneys included. Other organs are numbered and arranged around it. Before cutting out the figures on this page, [Vesalius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vesalius "Vesalius") suggests that readers glue the page onto parchment and gives instructions on how to assemble the pieces and paste the multilayered figure onto a base "muscle man" illustration. "Epitome", fol.14a. HMD Collection, WZ 240 V575dhZ 1543.

The earliest known writings on the circulatory system are found in the [Ebers Papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus "Ebers Papyrus") (16th century BCE), an [ancient Egyptian medical papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine "Ancient Egyptian medicine") containing over 700 prescriptions and remedies, both physical and spiritual. In the [papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Papyrus "Papyrus"), it acknowledges the connection of the heart to the arteries. The Egyptians thought air came in through the mouth and into the lungs and heart. From the heart, the air travelled to every member through the arteries. Although this concept of the circulatory system is only partially correct, it represents one of the earliest accounts of scientific thought.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In the 6th century BCE, the knowledge of circulation of vital fluids through the body was known to the [Ayurvedic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayurveda "Ayurveda") physician [Sushruta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sushruta "Sushruta") in [ancient India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_India "History of India").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Dwivedi&Dwivedi07-31) He also seems to have possessed knowledge of the arteries, described as 'channels' by Dwivedi & Dwivedi (2007).[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Dwivedi&Dwivedi07-31) The first major ancient Greek research into the circulatory system was completed by Plato in the _Timaeus_, who argues that blood circulates around the body in accordance with the general rules that govern the motions of the elements in the body; accordingly, he does not place much importance in the heart itself.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-32) The [valves of the heart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_valve "Heart valve") were discovered by a physician of the [Hippocrat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippocrates "Hippocrates")ic school around the early 3rd century BC.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-33) However, their function was not properly understood then. Because blood pools in the veins after death, arteries look empty. Ancient anatomists assumed they were filled with air and that they were for the transport of air.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

The [Greek physician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_Medicine "Ancient Greek Medicine"), [Herophilus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herophilus "Herophilus"), distinguished veins from arteries but thought that the [pulse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulse "Pulse") was a property of arteries themselves. Greek anatomist [Erasistratus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erasistratus "Erasistratus") observed that arteries that were cut during life bleed. He ascribed the fact to the phenomenon that air escaping from an artery is replaced with blood that enters between veins and arteries by very small vessels. Thus he apparently postulated capillaries but with reversed flow of blood.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In 2nd-century AD [Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rome "Rome"), the [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_Medicine "Ancient Greek Medicine") physician [Galen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galen "Galen") knew that blood vessels carried blood and identified venous (dark red) and arterial (brighter and thinner) blood, each with distinct and separate functions. Growth and energy were derived from venous blood created in the liver from chyle, while arterial blood gave vitality by containing pneuma (air) and originated in the heart. Blood flowed from both creating organs to all parts of the body where it was consumed and there was no return of blood to the heart or liver. The heart did not pump blood around, the heart's motion sucked blood in during diastole and the blood moved by the pulsation of the arteries themselves.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_] Galen believed that the arterial blood was created by venous blood passing from the left ventricle to the right by passing through 'pores' in the interventricular septum, air passed from the lungs via the pulmonary artery to the left side of the heart. As the arterial blood was created 'sooty' vapors were created and passed to the lungs also via the pulmonary artery to be exhaled.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In 1025, _[The Canon of Medicine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Canon\_of\_Medicine "The Canon of Medicine")_ by the [Persian physician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_Medicine "Ancient Iranian Medicine"), [Avicenna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avicenna "Avicenna"), "erroneously accepted the Greek notion regarding the existence of a hole in the ventricular septum by which the blood traveled between the ventricles." Despite this, Avicenna "correctly wrote on the [cardiac cycles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiac_cycle "Cardiac cycle") and valvular function", and "had a vision of blood circulation" in his _Treatise on Pulse_.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-34) While also refining Galen's erroneous theory of the pulse, Avicenna provided the first correct explanation of pulsation: "Every beat of the pulse comprises two movements and two pauses. Thus, expansion: pause: contraction: pause. [...] The pulse is a movement in the heart and arteries ... which takes the form of alternate expansion and contraction."[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-Chamsi-Pasha2014-35)

In 1242, the [Arabian physician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medicine_in_medieval_Islam "Medicine in medieval Islam"), [Ibn al-Nafis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis "Ibn al-Nafis") described the process of [pulmonary circulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation "Pulmonary circulation") in greater, more accurate detail than his predecessors, though he believed, as they did, in the notion of vital spirit ([pneuma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pneuma "Pneuma")), which he believed was formed in the left ventricle. Ibn al-Nafis stated in his _Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon_:[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-West_2008-36)

> ...the blood from the right chamber of the heart must arrive at the left chamber but there is no direct pathway between them. The thick septum of the heart is not perforated and does not have visible pores as some people thought or invisible pores as Galen thought. The blood from the right chamber must flow through the vena arteriosa (pulmonary artery) to the lungs, spread through its substances, be mingled there with air, pass through the arteria venosa ([pulmonary vein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulmonary_vein "Pulmonary vein")) to reach the left chamber of the heart and there form the vital spirit...

In addition, Ibn al-Nafis had an insight into what later became a larger theory of the [capillary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capillary "Capillary") circulation. He stated that "there must be small communications or pores (_manafidh_ in Arabic) between the pulmonary artery and vein," a prediction that preceded the discovery of the capillary system by more than 400 years.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-West_2008-36) Ibn al-Nafis' theory was confined to blood transit in the lungs and did not extend to the entire body.

[Michael Servetus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Servetus "Michael Servetus") was the first European to describe the function of pulmonary circulation, although his achievement was not widely recognized at the time, for a few reasons. He firstly described it in the "Manuscript of Paris"[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-37)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-38) (near 1546), but this work was never published. And later he published this description, but in a theological treatise, _Christianismi Restitutio_, not in a book on medicine. Only three copies of the book survived but these remained hidden for decades, the rest were burned shortly after its publication in 1553 because of persecution of Servetus by religious authorities.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

A better known discovery of pulmonary circulation was by [Vesalius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vesalius "Vesalius")'s successor at [Padua](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Padua "University of Padua"), [Realdo Colombo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realdo_Colombo "Realdo Colombo"), in 1559.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

[![Image 33](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/250px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg)

Image of veins from [William Harvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Harvey "William Harvey")'s _[Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exercitatio\_Anatomica\_de\_Motu\_Cordis\_et\_Sanguinis\_in\_Animalibus "Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus")_, 1628

Finally, the English physician [William Harvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Harvey "William Harvey"), a pupil of [Hieronymus Fabricius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hieronymus_Fabricius "Hieronymus Fabricius") (who had earlier described the valves of the veins without recognizing their function), performed a sequence of experiments and published his _[Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exercitatio\_Anatomica\_de\_Motu\_Cordis\_et\_Sanguinis\_in\_Animalibus "Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus")_ in 1628, which "demonstrated that there had to be a direct connection between the venous and arterial systems throughout the body, and not just the lungs. Most importantly, he argued that the beat of the heart produced a continuous circulation of blood through minute connections at the extremities of the body. This is a conceptual leap that was quite different from Ibn al-Nafis' refinement of the anatomy and bloodflow in the heart and lungs."[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_system#cite_note-39) This work, with its essentially correct exposition, slowly convinced the medical world. However, Harvey did not identify the capillary system connecting arteries and veins; this was discovered by [Marcello Malpighi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcello_Malpighi "Marcello Malpighi") in 1661.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

--------

*   [Cardiovascular drift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiovascular_drift "Cardiovascular drift")
*   [Cardiac cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiac_cycle "Cardiac cycle")– Performance of the human heart
*   [Vital heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vital_heat "Vital heat")
*   [Cardiac muscle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardiac_muscle "Cardiac muscle")– Muscular tissue of heart in vertebrates
*   [Major systems of the human body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Major_systems_of_the_human_body "Major systems of the human body")– Collective structure of a human being Pages displaying short descriptions of redirect targets
*   [Amato Lusitano](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amato_Lusitano "Amato Lusitano")– Portuguese physician (1511–1568)Pages displaying short descriptions of redirect targets
*   [Vascular resistance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vascular_resistance "Vascular resistance")– Force from blood vessels that affects blood flow
